RATING: 9
I have rare praise (for me) for this game.  First, I really like Martin Wallace's wargames, but I have not liked his Eurogames, but this is an exception.  It has a good theme and strong mechanics.  It is not an easy game, but it is not overwrought with too many components to optimize.  You make money: plain and simple and the one who gets the most wins.  You build cars and sell them and people want the latest and greatest.  Pretty straight-forward.

This is pretty much the top end of "complexity" I want in a Eurogame.  The reason why is because I think the "complex" ones (and I use that phrase loosely) just attempts to create another optimization puzzle with more and more parts to optimize and I find that dreadfully dull.  When I want complex, I play wargames, so I have can have complexity with meaning, theme, and purpose.
The game is 4 turns long and each turn has 3 actions for each player to take.  Each action is one of five choices (and the 5th is basically "pass") so while the perfect strategy is not apparent in your first play, you can understand what is happening and you can quickly learn from your mistakes for the next game.

Kuddos to Martin Wallace.  This one is worthy to place alongside your wargames!

RATING: 9.3
Not that I've played a great many, but this is the best Martin Wallace game I have ever played. And visually,  it's an absolutely gorgeous game (at least in the Mayfair edition: other versions may prove to be graphically ugly)!
I have exceedingly little interest in the early American auto industry, yet Automobile has proven to be a winner for me. An extremely tense economic game that requires considerable attention to every little thing that everyone does. It's a reasonable facsimile of a real world economy, but it's still a euro. If you want a true economy in a box, play Container. 
The uncertainty as to the demand for automobiles every turn adds just enough randomness to the game to keep it from being even remotely dry. 
One of the best brain-burners I have ever played; and an AP prone player's heaven. It's better not to mention to them that you own this one!
My only major complaint is the complete and utter lack of player aids, for which the game is crying out. Their absence is a crime which must be brought before the Game Crimes Tribunal in Essen.

RATING: 5
3-5(5)

It's a really tough game to explain and, despite the fact that you only execute twelve actions throughout the game (four of which are inevitably producing cars), there's a lot of thinking and fiddling.

So, the game mechanisms and dynamics actually work pretty well. You do need to be constantly upgrading your factories and closing down old factories and there is jockeying to sell your cars and such.

However, there are actually only twelve actions (with, granted, other decisions such as what role to take, how many cars to make, which executive decision to select, etc.), which does seem a little thin. On top of that, it's pretty abstract and a bit convoluted, especially regarding how and when you get to sell cars. Further, there's fiddliness abound regarding having to sell the cars (sequentially) and loss cubes and all that. Also, the relative arbitrariness of the demand tiles, which can be backbreaking depending on how they turn out.

I think the game has a lot of clever mechanical elements for a market simulation and all that but the game flow feels a bit scripted (e.g. at which points do certain cars see demand and what demand they'll find) and the fiddliness diminishes the payoff.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I've had some fun playing Automobile.  Unknown demand gives the game some delicious angst, but I think it is also problematic.  With four or five players, your single demand tile for two varieties of car tells you nothing meaningful about total demand.  So either you play the percentages or you gamble on freakish demand.  

There is a lot of maths in Automobile; figuring out average demand, counting little cars produced, subtracting distributors, not forgetting Howard's two sales, figuring out what all that means for you.  That's rather more calculation than I enjoy. 

Automobile has some nice, thematic elements and a greater degree of player interaction than some give it credit for. The surest way of winning seems to be to dominate production of one type of car.  Other players can certainly mess with that strategy.

After four games I question Automobile's replay value. Its systems feel very constrained, the scope for exploring alternative strategies quite limited.

RATING: 3
I suppose this would be considered a "hardcore Eurogame." What I found is that I didn't get a whole lot of satisfaction from playing it. It's similar to a game in the same way that baseball is similar to a sport.

Let me expand on that little comparison:

Baseball as a sport is largely boring. There's a lot of downtime and athleticism doesn't play a HUGE part in the game. However, when baseball becomes interesting is when you break it down into its components. Baseball is a game made up of hundreds of minigames. There's the mini-game where the pitcher keeps an eye on the runner on first while attempting to make a pitch (or decides to pick off the runner on first). There's the mini-game between the pitcher and batter. There's the mini-game between the manager and the opposing pitcher - determining the correct batting order. The list can go on extensively, but I feel I've made my point.

Automobile is largely the same. You have the mini-game of choosing which "personality" you want. You've got the mini-game of trying to one-up your opponents on having the most recent factory. The game of trying to guess how much demand there will be for any given pigeonhole, and so on.

The difference is if I sit down to watch baseball and begin drinking heavily, I'll start becoming really entertained in heckling the pitcher/batter/umpire/peanut vendor. If I were to do the same in Automobile, I'd get kicked out of whoever's place I was gaming at. 

There's the possibility I didn't really give this game a fair shake. I'd probably try it once more. I feel that there probably is some pretty intense strategy that goes into it once you've played it 9 or 10 times, but it's so abstracted with regards to what's a "good" play and what's a "bad" play that it's hard to stay interested.

And now it seems I've written a novel on a game I've only scored a 3.

RATING: 7
Mások véleménye alapján ennél számolgatósabb játékra számítottam. És kevésbé szórakoztatóra. Vagyis kellemes meglepetés volt!
Számolgatós igen, de nem abnormálisan. A kereslet bizonytalansági tényező, de nagyjából lehet vele kalkulálni. Az a fajta játék, ahol egész kis forgatókönyv ír le egy fordulót:) Szerencsére az újabb kiadáson szemléletes ábrák utalnak az egyes lépésekre. A kivitelezés egyébként is nagyon profi (itt a korábbi kiadás fa alkatrészei helyett vastag lapkák dominálnak).
A játék elég nagy része teljesen egyedi, általam ismert más játékokhoz nem hasonlítható mechanizmusokból áll. Végig nem lehet tudni, ki áll nyerésre. Nagyon szűkös a pénz, de nem szabad megijedni a kölcsön felvételétől (Megjegyzés: tetszik, hogy ilyen sokféle címletet adtak a játékhoz...). Nagyon figyelni kell, hiszen összesen 12 akciónk van a 3-4 óra során (nekünk ennyi volt 5 fővel). 1 elhibázott, vagy akár csak balszerencsés akció meghatározhatja a végeredményt. A 6 karakter közti választás teszi igazán sokszínűvé a palettát. Így az első játék után legalább 3-4 féle alapvetően különböző stratégia jutott eszembe, mindnek megvan a maga kockázata, de szerintem mindegyik esélyt kínál a győzelemre. Ez az újrajátszhatóság mellett szól! Egy tanulság, amit levontam: a gyárak bezárásával csínján kell bánni, bár néha muszáj, és előnyökkel is jár, de azért fixen elég sokat bukunk rajta.
Összességében jó játék, máskor is játszanék vele, de azért nem az a bármikor előveszem kategória.
---
A 2. játék után kezd körvonalazódni egy a többinél erősebb stratégia (sok kis gyár...). Ha ez így van, az nem éppen pozitívum, de majd még tesztelni kell.

RATING: 7
This is a very interesting economic game, with an intriguing theme and well integrated mechanics (the obsolescence and R&D are simple but very elegant rules).
Player's have only 12 actions to create a business by producing and selling cars during the first decades of mass automobile production, and the low number of actions and turns seems a good tension generator.
Oddily, the quantity of available stuff is the same with 3, 4 or 5 players, so with less than the full 5 players the tension is inferior.
This needs more plays for a complete evaluation, but at first sight could be another hit from Wallace...

RATING: 4
Automobile reminds me of Martin Wallace's Tinners' Trail.  In Tinners' Trail players have to guess when to sell.  The rate of return is based on a die roll.  If you guessed correctly, you will probably win.  In Automobile players have to guess how many cars to produce.  The number of produced cars to be sold each round is based on a tile draw.  If you guessed correctly, you will probably win.

RATING: 10
After 3 plays this is what I think:

Fun 10/10
Theme vs reality 10/10
Player interaction 9/10
Last turn tension 9/10
Look 10/10
Originality 10/10
Replayability 10/10
Rules 10/10 intuitive
Sweetspot 5 players 10/10, 4 player 9/10, 3 player 8/10

This is an amazing game. It is economic war in a box. It plays smoothly. Those in your group that are walking calculators will love this game however they are not guarenteed a win because there is the slightest of unknown in the game which means they need to have more than a calculator. They must have intuition and business savvy. There are several layers to this game that make it a joy to replay and will not be visible on your first play. This game is worth owning even if you own Indonesia. IMHO Automobile is a much better economic game than Wealth of Nations or Containers, such that I won't even play those games again. My collection is about owning 10's and 9 not 8s and below.

This game is a gamers game however it is easily teachable to someone who has graduated from gateway games. In other words this would be an excellent economic game for the intermediate gamer.

Time it takes to play is excellent for the what the game gives back in fun, tension and experience. Martin Wallace has proven himself again to be an excellent designer with this game.

I love this game, it is worth the investment and I recommend it to gamers, Wallace Fans and players that are finished with gateway games. 

In short, Automobile is a big game with many little mechanics that work together. It's an exercise in manipulation of turn order and precedence to try and work production and sales together but apart from financial opportunity cost in paying to build unsalable cars the stream of loss cubes from bad decisions doesn't seem so bad. With all the mechanics and special actions and turn order mechanics combined it's a little difficult to get a complete picture of where things are, but there aren't that many really big pointy corners waiting to catch you.

Session report [url]
http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/417306[/url] 
Session report conclusion
Despite my poor performance in the critical third turn I’m totally enamored with this game. I haven’t been able to stop thinking about it, and I can’t wait to play again. All of the mechanisms really mesh together well to simulate the feeling of running a business (if not completely thematically, you at least feel like you’re making critical decisions that go straight to your bottom line). 

The four turns are structurally identical but each feels different than the last because the relative demand is adjusted between each turn, and players are ramping up their production. Even within a turn the tension really builds, as you try to decide how much to produce, then how to possibly sell everything given what everyone else has produced. When those demand tiles are flipped over it’s actually a tense moment.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Another excellent offering from Martin Wallace. I happen to like Martin's approach to theme implementation, although I know some others find it maddening. The inter-relationships seem intuitive enough that I don't find myself needing to rationalize how everything works to appreciate the game.

Players invest in three categories of automobile (cheap, mid-range, and luxury) and in progressively newer versions of each. You have to balance outlay (cost of factories and cost of auto production) versus how many cars of the various types you hope to sell. Whoever has the most money at the end of the game wins.

Does it all make sense? Probably not if you put it all under a microscope, but in game terms it works the way you would expect. Cheap car factories cost less, the cars they produce also cost less to make, and you can churn out a lot of them - but they don't sell for very much. Luxury car factories are the most expensive as is the per car cost of production but they sell for a lot more - however the number of cars each factory produces is very low in comparison. Naturally, the mid-range car factories are between the two. The kicker is that the demand for each type of car is not exactly known, but there are a couple of ways to sell cars that mitigates that aspect of the equation.

The bottom line is that each turn (and there are only four), you are guessing how many cars of each type the market will absorb, and how many of these categories the other players will concentrate on. Based on these factors you will plan your own investments. Miscalculate by too much and you will suffer stiff financial penalties. Guess right and you will make out like a bandit! The cool part is that your calculations are not done entirely in the dark - but surprises can happen.

Once again, Martin delivers a game that reveals more each time you play and experience will be a definite asset. There will be a definite learning curve but I look forward to it!

RATING: 4
Played 5 times and I'm done with this now.

My main problems with the game are: 

Random Market - dull and a huge element of luck that I don't want in this sort of game. Yes you have partial information but it is of little use, as you have to assume others are making their choices based on their information, so in the end it's a shot in the dark unless you know the other players very well.

Player interaction - yes there's some (mostly incidental), but not as much as I'd like.

Biggest decision in the game is turn order - and everthing follows after that. 

I end the game feeling it would be ok as a 1/2 hour game but not 2 hours - there's not enough game, by which I mean meaningful/critical decisions and enjoyment (rather than bean counting and AP).

RATING: 6
I got Automobile for two reasons: it seemed like Martin Wallace in the vein I love best (that is, the Brass vein), and it was an interesting theme.  Of course we intended to play primarily with two, and the existence of a variant on the 'geek was incentive enough.  So, I managed to snag it for a good price from a shop in Great Britain, and after 8 weeks of anticipation, it finally arrived.  Our first Treefrog, our first MW signature... (sigh).

And how is it, you may ask?  Well, with the 2 player variant, it was not good at all.  This is NOT a game that can be adjusted for two in the same way Brass can.  There was no tension, no decisions, no reason to build new factories.  Basically, I came to understand that this game is ALL about chaos, trying to make the best with limited information, and the claustrophobia brought on by multiple players.  With 4? It is not great. Ok fun, but nothing exceptional. It is not overly long, though, so worth giving at try, especially if you like MW designs.

RATING: 7
Yet another heavy economic game from Martin Wallace. It's really a brilliant design, streamlined and tight, but this is just not my type of game. At one point on my turn I did nothing but sit in silence, for five minutes, counting. I'm sure I made the game about as fun for the other players as it was for me, but for some reason I'm incapable of playing these kinds of games without trying to micromanage my money.

More plays would probably make me more comfortable with the mechanics such that I wouldn't need to calculate every decision so precisely, but I don't know how often I'll want to play this. I tend to prefer games where you can play intuitively, not analytically. Everyone in my gaming group loves these number-crunching games, however, and they are all much better at them than I am. I guess I'll just suck it up and keep playing. Sigh.

RATING: 9
It's an economic Wallace - amongst my favourite type of games - and probably one of his best.  Unlike Brass, this is a game where the connection between actions is apparent after reading the rules but the extra twists added by executive decisions in particular add a nice layer of decision making. Now looking forward to playing with five - but with experienced players only I think else it might be a tad slow.

RATING: 2
What an awful game. I cannot be said to be a fan of Martin Wallace games of course, but I usually enjoy them enough for a play or two. But Automobile has to be his most wretched offering in quite some time, far beyond simply being a little clunky or inelegant; after about the halfway mark it was pure agony to play. Overwhelming, gratuitous complexity, complete themelessness and total opacity, and a tendency to get bogged down in extreme AP ... it was just no fun at all. And the game has the classic Martin Wallace traits of significant imbalance, in that it gives you the illusion of many choices but in practice only a few are valid, but it's an excruciating amount of work to figure out which those valid choices are.

It also doesn't help that this is an almost complete retread. It's just bits of Brass, bits of Age of Steam, bits of Tinner's Trail, all thrown together with little regard for aesthetics or coherence. There is startlingly little original thought on display here. If, like me, you value some small amount of artistry in games, avoid Automobile like the plague.

On balance, this went far beyond the indifference  to cordial dislike I usually feel for Wallace games; I actively hated this game. It's so completely overwrought for no purpose whatsoever.

RATING: 4
This game made me realize that, although I'm a huge eurogamer, I just don't care that much for economic games. Basically I find them to be dry and frankly outright boring. I loooove eurogames, but these economic games are simply not any fun. At all.

Now, I have only played this game once, so take my comment with a grain of salt. We were all new to this, and we played suboptimally (for instance, one player sold a cheap car factory after the second round, right before the demand for cheap cars went up, and there weren't many factories for those cars in the first place...) Also, we played very carefully, so almost all of our produced cars went on to be sold. So I admit that we were all playing poorly, but I don't want to spend two hours playing this again when I can easily manage a play of my favorite or second-favorite game in that time (Agricola and Terra Mystica). Time is just too precious to play a game I don't like. Even though we played poorly, it seems we played equally bad as three of the four players ended within $100 of each other! So the game seems quite tight, but I'm always a little disappointed when the game requires you to take a loan in order to do well (the only player who didn't take a loan ended up $500 behind the rest). Oh, and I'd like to stress that I don't agree with the complaint that the demand tiles are too random; rather, I'd say that they have minimal impact. And even though you have, for instance, a 2 and a 3, you don't really have much information as the other players will likely have higher tiles; it seemed that the outcome was the average most of the times.

RATING: 9.5
Tight, tight, tight.  Everything about this game is tight.  A well designed game that goes from point A to point B to point C with exact precision, but without being predictable.  Choices that you make at the begining of each round are so very important to determining your course of action later in the round.

This game is also a brain-burner.  Lots of thinking and planning going on.  There is also a great level of player screwage - not enough to make your opponent hate you and never want to play again, but just enough to gain those few extra points that you need to win the game.

An excellent MW design, and probably one of my favorites.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A very good Wallace game. This one definitely wears you out after playing it, kind of like Age of Steam.  Quite a heavy brain burner - but there is a lot fun in the mechanics.  There is a race to build better cars, but only because the better cars have first dibs in the marketplace. If your car factories become too obsolete, then you are forced to pay a lot in losses or forced into closing it down.  And trying to find ways to expand and shrink your company, all while also trying to find ways to get into the market demand is quite a challenge considering your limited number of actions.  Add in a special roles phase and a special power phase (executive decisions) and it all balances nicely into a thoroughly engaging game.

RATING: 9
:sugar: :( :( :) :) :D 

UPDATE 9/15: Automobile is a fine game for four, but with five it really sings, to such an extent that playing with fewer players feels like a missed opportunity. When the number of players is maxed out, you really get an interesting diversity of strategies in place, and real tension in the role selection and executive decision phases. If the game felt excessively procedural/on rails when you've played in the past with 3 or 4 players, do yourself a favor and try it with the maximum player count.

---

A great, underrated game. Sort of a combination of Brass without a map and 18xx boiled down to the train rush. Like many of my favorite games (Brass, Le Havre, etc), you must build the latest and greatest technologies to win, but the very act of your building opens up new opportunities for your fellow players. Definitely better as a game with more than three as the three player game (in my limited experience) tends to lead to players focusing on a single market segment. Downside of more players is that you have even less information about demand than you do in the three player game. Accustomed as I am to games of business implying perfect information (or near enough to same), the randomness of the demand tile draws irks a little [i]but[/i] there are ways for the conservative player to mitigate the impact of randomness and, well, sometimes, you have to throw caution to the wind and say "who dares wins."

RATING: 8
I'm a big fan of all the Treefrog stuff so far.  Automobile is a fun and somewhat opaque (strategy-wise) economic game with some elements of Steel Driver, Brass and Tinner's Trail. 

It has a feel all its own. There is no obvious winning strategy. Seems like multiple things could be profitable, and could lead to victory.

Its not clear to me how much of strategy needs to be planned....and how much each action is a tactical choice based on what other players do. Feels about like Steel Driver in that regard. What is clear is that you can do a lot to affect other players....and it gives you a lot to think about with each move.

This is one that I think could go up in rating...and I look forward to more plays. I'm very glad I own a copy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I'm not sure how I feel about this game after the first attempt. We were up and running moderately quickly, which for Wallace titles is saying something; the fact that there were no Brass-like rules exceptions all over the place helped considerably. The game has a good feel of gearing up, maintaining the status quo and then preparing for the end. And with the exception of two small quibbles the 999 Games-edition was looking gorgeous. But there were cons too: the game is 'mathy' (which I don't mind, but do not particularly enjoy either), and it is bordering on the edge of my tolerability for player chaos owing to a substantial lack of random influences as well as tolerability for freedom of choice owing to a lack of options for the last player in the turn order. Turn order is important at certain moments in the game but not in others, thus making it difficult to decide whether to play for turn order on a given moment. You can probably get away with one or two hiatuses in your action flow, but certainly not more. Finally the market demand, which is decided by random tile draw feels at odds with the precise clockwork nature of the rest of the game. Yes, it constitutes a random factor, but it is a factor you have little hope of negating if you guess wrong.

I would certainly not be opposed to playing the game anew, possibly even several times, but at the same time I'm not convinced the game would click sufficiently to make it last. I guess the word I'm looking for is 'indifference'.

[b]Update[/b] Second play, feelings remain quite similar.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a very dense game of building an economic engine, following market trends, and carefully managing your cash flow.  It is the most complex game I think I've learned since Vinhos, but like that game, once you get a grip on the rules, it starts to all flow together.  What will take time is trying to get a grip on a real strategy.  With so many choices, it's hard to see a clear path right away.

Players set up factories to produce cars in 3 different market segments, then produce those cars and attempt to sell them - either through salesmen or through market demand.  Staying behind the current technological trends or not selling your cars will cost you - and those costs add up over time.

I definitely like this game - and I think with time I might even love it.  But I need more plays to figure that out.

RATING: 8
Played at BGG.Con.  Admittedly, by this time I was no longer drunk, but hung over. 

Very interesting economic game of the history of the early years of the automotive industry. I really quite liked how the game modelled two different marketing approaches- either go for perceived 'quality', and go for high end cars, but small amounts of them. Or you could just go 'Mass Market' and try and flood the market with your cheaper cars.  But if you do that, you have to be able to get them to the market, with dealers, price incentives, and the like.

Very good game. I'm not a big Martin Wallace fan, but I really enjoyed this game and I've been trying to score a copy of this to play again.  Perhaps after a game without being hungover, I'll raise my rating of it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Players are automobile entrepreneurs in early America.  Players over four turns spend actions to construct factories, build cars and invest in research and development.  All this costs money, and you hopefully turn a profit by selling all your cars by the end of the turn.  Unsold cars, unused dealers, and redundant factories incur loss points, which equates to an escalating cash penalty.  Most cash at the end of four turns wins.  I've come to the conclusion that while I admire Martin's designs, and are happy to try them once, I seldom need to go through the effort of playing them again (this pretty much goes back to Stockers!) - Steam and Steel Driver are the exceptions.  Automobile joins that stack of games.  It's probably good, I enjoyed my one playing of it, pressing all the game mechanic buttons and levers, but ultimately it was just a business game of wooden bits, dressed up with some illustrations of cars and people.  Take that away and it was all pretty dry stuff.  I also got the impression it came down to "guessing" how many cars you could sell each turn, surrounded by lots of mechanical fluff.  Great if you want a meaty business game, otherwise be careful.

RATING: 8
Classic Wallace, with a simple ruleset but complex interaction. During your first game, you might not grasp clearly what every one of your limited action have as an impact, but it will leave you thinking back on the game and seeing how you could have improved your score.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Yet another improvement in Wallace's long line of great competitive economic games. Jockey for position in newer and newer models of cars, each newer plant you create nails the owners of older plants with more and more penalties. How to assure your cars get sold, and how to make a profit, are rough in this challenging economic game.

One factor that sets this one apart from many other are the vast number of incremental, meaningful decisions. A game like 1830 has lots of turns but a handful of hugely important, brittle events. This game has many more decisions that are all meaningful, and in sum add up to your progress yet one decision rarely sinks you. Thus, a single mistake will rarely cost you a game of misery. 

Highly recommended, if this sort of agressive economic game appeals to you!

RATING: 4
I'm a big Martin Wallace fan in general, so it pains me that I don't like this one. I've only played once but every now and then I check out the game's page again to see if perhaps my opinion of it should change. Every time I come to the same conclusion: it's just not that interesting of a game. The few decisions that you have are made or broken by how well you guess at the demand each turn (after a lot of calculator keypad mashing). Push your luck and win, and it's a great day for you. Hold back a little bit, and you did OK but lost out on a major opportunity that may not come again this game. But it's all guesswork in any case.

Seems that deciding which car line to produce and which bonus power to take each turn are the interesting aspects of the game, but the rest is AP-inducing number crunching and guesswork. I've heard that the game is best played by people not afraid to gamble and lose, but it's too long and otherwise too strategic of an endeavor for the game to hinge on that type of venture.

RATING: 2
(3-5 players) Uhhhh...I bought this based on a comment that said we should enjoy this if we were disappointed by Tinner's Trail being too loose of a game. Played one game and feel like it's WAY more complicated but ultimately is just as loose. In a four player game, demand was never a problem and no one was particularly compelled to exercise Executive Decisions. Everyone did pretty much as you would expect with their 3 actions each round: build factory, produce cars, place distributors. WTF else would you be doing? It just feels like a mess without any tension. I think I'm done with Wallace games for a while. The Treefrog games seem cool (and you want them to be cool) but they're just not delivering.

Update: After reading some more, it seems like this game only clicks if everyone is going all out on production, thus making demand scarce and making executive decisions for selling cars more compelling. I can't think of any other game that requires *everyone* in the game to play a very particular way for core game mechanics (and interesting game play) to kick in. What a ridiculous design.

RATING: 7
I really wanted to LOVE Automobile, being a pretty big Martin Wallace fan (especially of Brass, Liberte, Age of Steam, and Perikles).  But in the end, I only like it.  I’m not a huge fan of economic games, but this is a good one within that genre.  With only 12 “actions” each player can take within the whole game, it’s hard to see how this could take about 3 hours to play with 4 players, but that’s what happened in our first game.  As many others have said, it’s very mathy, to the point that it should really come with a calculator for each player. I was surprised at  how scripted it felt to me, as it doesn’t give me the sense that there are as many options as there should be for a gamer’s game with this esteemed reputation.  But I freely acknowledge that the strategy is probably a lot deeper and more subtle than first made itself apparent to me.  I like the theme and it gave me and my children some good motivation to do some research on the famous men featured in the game. My kids enjoyed this significantly more than I did, and I’m sure I’ll be indulging them with another play soon.

RATING: 6
Pretty tight economic game. When compared with Brass, the number of options available (and thus also the number of special rules and exceptions) has been trimmed down a lot, making Automobile a much more streamlined and polished experience. Even the few actions available allow for very meaningful (and excruciating) decisions but I'm wondering if the replayability might suffer a bit as a result.

Update: Actually, it turns out the game's most grating issue is not so much replayability as it is incalculable demand. You just can't make a properly informed decision when deciding how much to produce. If you play conservatively and produce few cars you just invite others to overproduce and get away with it. If you overproduce yourself you might just get lucky or, assuming the other players don't let themselves be scared away, everyone will suffer. In any case it feels like the winner is not determined by skillful play but by guessing the demand most accurately. Downgrading to a 6.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is my lowest rated Martin Wallace game. It involves so much mental math my friends and I had to get out a calculator by the end of the game. Why would he do this to us?

I love how players position themselves along the track and invest in that position by building more than one factory there or a parts factory. I love how cars are sold, around the track but divided by the different markets, and how advertising and discounts can change the rate at which you sell. The salesmen are a little weird, but in the end I love that mechanic too.

I just won't be able to bring it to the table unless everyone in my gaming group is well rested, well fed, and ready to revisit their times tables from elementary school.

RATING: 4
Automobile is an economic game with little hidden information. If you wanted to take the time, you could calculate the expected profit of each action. While you don't know exactly how much demand there will be, or who (if anyone) will advertise, you could take each possibility into account, along with its probabilty, and get a range of expected values. 

I didn't want to slow the game down by doing these calculations, nor did I enjoy playing sub-optimally, so this isn't really a game (or even a class of games) that I'll go out of my way to play again. There's nothing wrong with Automobile as an economic game, it's simply not my cup of tea.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Maybe this is a little too harsh a rating, as it is one of Wallace's more streamlined offerings but the overall impression after my first outing is of a game that is inoffensive yet terribly average.  

Automobile is stripped down to the essential mechanics (unlike most of Wallace's offerings), and they all mesh together well creating smooth gameplay, however there really doesn't seem to be much in the way of decision making here.  There also lacks any twist, so play feels repetitive and limited. 

There are only twelve actions in the game, 4 of which are going to be production.  This leaves 8 actions to each player, of which you have three choices - take a couple research cubes, place 1-3 distributors, or buy/close a factory.  Chances are players will spend one turn each round buying a factory.  So we are now at 4 actions.

Buying factories is not really too difficult a decision.  You buy the best you can in which ever market you perceive to be most beneficial.  I don't see any dilemma ever presenting itself here.

And this is where my issue with Automobile lies.  The decisions are all fairly obvious, and bend around the unknown demand of each vehicle.  So players guess, and most of the time play conservatively as the penalty for risk is often way too overwhelming to warrant attempting. THe only true decisions seem to come from choosing a role at the beginning of each round, as there powers timed right seem to really make the difference.

---the following is an opinion in the working---

I will go out on a limb and really stretch my point of view and say Automobile feels like Galaxy Trucker to me in a way. Not in mechanics persay, but in the result of how the model of play in each game is set up.

In both games you make a couple decisions to set up your turn, and then you let the fate of industry take it's course.  Both games offer small decisions upfront, and bottom load the second half of each play round with maintenance.  After the dust settles you see where you sit for the following round and repeat.  

Yes the decision tree is vastly different, but the scope of play feels the same.  In Galaxy trucker you can get a sense of disasters arising on the horizon from sneaking a peak at cards, while in Automobile you have a single demand tile. You then set up your business (or ship) to deal with the storm ahead.

With a game this seemingly light I prefer the spatial elements and not to seriousness of Galaxy Trucker.

Very clever design - lots of complexity for a relatively straightforward system.  first game was challenging, looking forward to more to get to grips with the system.  Around Power Grid in weight - not as complex as Brass.

RATING: 8
I'll take the seemingly contrary view and say I prefer Ships to Automobile. The two games have a somewhat similar upgrade mechanic and  out-of-date loss mechanic. But that's more or less where the comparisons stop. The meat of the game comes in guessing demand as well as meeting that demand before your opponents do while also forcing them to take losses for overproducing. It's a fine rope to walk and one that makes for a really fascinating gaming experience. For better or worse, turn order is crucial in this game.

PROS:
-Good interaction & tough decisions
-Relatively short playing time and straightforward rules

CONS:
-Ugly as sin

RATING: 7
5th game played as part of my experience of the Great Canadian Board Game Blitz in Ottawa.  By the time this hit the table, I was feeling a little gamed out, and having just finished an excellent game of Goa, this one didn't stand a chance.  I could tell as the game was being explained to us, my opponents were feeling much in much the same.  The one player on my left (playing yellow) showed such disdain that he started asking if he could just pass as his first action...?  I suggested a move for him and he just did what I suggested.  The rest of the game continued in a similar fashion, where after I'd made some weak and/or terrible decisions for myself, I'd have a great suggestion for the yellow player and he'd capitalize on it.  He ended up winning and I came in dead dead last.  Funny how sometimes seeing an opponent's optimal strategy is easier than figuring out your own.  Maybe I'll just keep my mouth shut next time...
If I do get to play this one again, I think I'll enjoy it a lot more.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
While there are exceptions, I tend to not enjoy economic games.  I usually find them to be dry, too calculating, and just too much work.  While there is a fair amount of calculations necessary in Automobile, I find these to be exciting, and they have an often dynamic result on the game.  The swings of fortunate can be dramatic, with a slight over-or-under estimation in production spelling the difference between victory and defeat.  Tough decisions must be made throughout the entire game, and there appears to be a wide variety of strategies players can pursue.  This is a rich game that will challenge players game after game.  Sadly, if a player’s automobile company falters, there is no government bailout available.  Perhaps that will be an option in a future expansion!

As someone who is averse to economic games, I loved this one, which was a very big surprise. After my first (5-player) I was itching to play it again, which stunned my gaming group.  It's a tremendous game. 

For the economic-game phobic, Automobile blends mechanics with such elegance and maintains such a taut and steady conflict/tension among the players, that this game transcends being "just" an economic game, if that makes sense. 

The true goal is to stay as efficient as possible with your resources - which so happen to be "money." It challenges players to choose the perfect moments to zig when others zag, and to take financial risks. The gaming experience is hot-blooded and visceral - this game is NOT a cold calculator or adding machine experience at all, which is why it shines for me (and why I usually dislike economic games).

Automobile distills and streamlines the economic game experience - and encloses it in an excellent, intuitive, and evocative theme so it's never dry and one is never too far asea with mere math, or rote spending and buying. 

I highly recommend this to someone who dislikes traditional financial/economic games where the object is to make the most money, because you'll find Automobile a revelation. Having the most $$$ is the object, but this game is, thankfully, NOT Acquire or Monopoly.

RATING: 8
Initially, the board and multiple steps make the rules feel difficult to play, but playing through a turn they become completely intuitive. Very few actions during the game makes each one important, other players choices influence what options/strategies are available as you build around the development track. Turn order variable and interestingly so - benefits to both ends of the track dependent on your position. The game is very financially minded, and working out expected profit from actions is important. The tile draw is a red herring though, as it really doesn't give you enough information to be relevant. Other than that as fine a game as I could have hoped. Not Brass, but then that wasn't what I was looking for. Our group will only call the cars Beep-Beeples.

RATING: 6.5
After my first play, I liked this game.  The final scores are high (in the $4000-$5000 range), so it's hard to figure out where all that money comes from, especially since you only have 12 actions during the game!  (Well, other than special actions tied to your investor.)

But there is one key point: you lose $100 for each factory (including tools factory) that you close down.  So you might just want to keep them around for a while.  On the other hand, you really don't want to collect lots of black cubes for old factories, even though your remaining factories at the end of the game are worth full points (no $100 penalty).  It's a tricky balance, especially since you don't know how the demand for cars is going to be allocated each turn.  In the last turn, having just enough distributors and the "sell 2 cars of your choice" investor is a good thing to help balance the randomness of the demand tiles -- you can use them to sell old cars from the factories at the end that might not ever get sold.

This game does need computerization -- it's hard to figure out exactly what combination of cars you can buy and sell with your current funds, and it's even harder to figure out how many factories to build on the right squares to leave you with enough funds to buy the range of cars required by that number of factories...

Resource management game, with not quite enough actions per turn. Based on limited information, funds & resources, maximize profit, without overproducing (which is penalized).

4th play 5-player solo- Wow! That made a big difference!
The character selection seemed harder and tighter, the choice to advance forward for plants more reasonable and the decision to do so more difficult.  Everything seemed to tighten up, a lot.  Raising rating to 7.75

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
All I can say is: Wow. Another hit from Martin Wallace. It is almost as good as Brass, in a different sense where it effectively captures the entrepreneur spirit of the early age of automobile manufacturing. There's no mistaking the sense of evolution of the American consumers' taste, going from initial small volume, niche market cars for the middle-class, to the cheap, mass-produced cars for the masses. In the background, there's also the luxury cars of limited production and limited demand. You've got to figure out how to make the most money in trying to satisfy the almost unpredictable consumer demand, similar to the Voice of the People in La Citta.

The trademark Wallace turn-order manipulation mechanic is there, with elements of Age of Steam and Tinner's Trail mixed in. It plays like Lokomotive Werks (and distantly like Bootleggers), with the advances of technology constantly making older factories more expensive to operate, and eventually making them obsolete. Four turns, with twelve actions seems like it's not enough to accomplish everything, but then there are the historically famous automobile executives to help you along. There are also many nail-biting decisions on building and closing factories, developing and expanding your distribution network (you can literally see the rise and fall of your dealerships, as the salesmen move around the three tiers of luxury, near-luxury, and Economy levels), how many to build, advertising, and discounting, doing everything you can to move your inventory. Otherwise, the cost of obsolescence, excess inventory, interest on loans, and layoffs will eat your profits. Exciting all around. Can't wait to play it again! Initial rating: '9'.

Update (09/27/11): It seems that this game has peaked, as nobody wants to play it anymore. There seems to be a feel that the game has a very narrow range of game play with the 16 actions/4 turns. The demand tile(s) you can see don't give you the complete picture of demand, although you could try to apply statistics--you can't. Somehow, you must go with your "gut" feel, and tailor your play within the context of the other players. There are some interesting tactical choices within a very limited framework, and some strategy, but when far removed from the game, it seems more like gambling--a crap shoot. If your gut is right, you win; if your instincts are wrong, you lose. This is probably why people are disinterested. I still like it, but the narrow play seems to have turned people off. I'm dropping it from a '9' to an '8'.

Update (01/15/12): Wow. This game has had a very short half-life. No one wants to play this due to the very narrow variance of impact, based on the players' few choices. It seems like it is min/max optimization for a $10 gain here or there. I liken it to a multi-player chess match, where you really can't go wrong with most of your choices, but it really boils down to who makes the most of what seem trivial mistakes. Despite its inviting design, it looks unforgiving from that regard. Perhaps that is why people avoid this. Dropping to a '7'.

Update (05/20/12) 10 plays: It's refreshing to play this again with a new group and new dynamics. However, it seems if one is aggressive enough, amidst a group of timid players, it is entirely possible to run away with the game.

Update (12/29/12) 14 plays: played against some of my friends who are relentless min-maxers, where one of them won twice. In both situations, the situation looked hopeless where the min-maxer took two loans, and even took a $400 hit for inefficiency, and still won by a few hundred dollars. Looks like the game still has subtleties to discover. However, it is discouraging when both parties can't figure out why they won or lost.

RATING: 7
Generally, this is a game of inches... I get $100 up on you here, you get $60 up on me there... etc...  However, bad play can quickly turn this into miles.  What this means is that failure to act by a poor player can hand the game to someone else.  Player chaos is really turned up here, so this game is best appreciated when everyone knows what they are doing.

To consider the impact of your choices, you generally have to add up a large amount of figures.  This reminds me of Power Grid and is a bit of a turn-off for me.  Despite this, there is something intriguing about the ruthless efficiency that you aspire to in this game, and it maintains my interest for the time being.

RATING: 7
A game with more moving parts than a Swiss watch. Expect plenty of new player confusion as you explain all the possible actions and their likely effects, but things will become much clearer after a round or two. RATING: 6
For me, this game just isn't very much fun. There are reasons for this: the theme doesn't engage me at all and I hate taking risks based on limited information, so I'm probably not the target audience here.  Much as I enjoy heavy economic games, Automobile for me is more stressful than fun. More detailed reflections below.

This game does an absolutely excellent job at inspiring a feeling of dread, if not outright terror.  In fact, I would go so far as to say it is the scariest game I have ever played. Arkham Horror has nothing on this. I guess this aspect of it is thematic...

It is very likely that, whatever you do, bad things will happen to your fledgling automobile company.  Your competitors will cause the customers to laugh at your cars.  You'll produce too many and find yourself deep in debt, or too few and fail to make any money. It is emphatically a game all about risk management, and as a rather risk averse person, I found it very stressful.  But it is a very clever game with a system that kept me thinking about our session for days, and I'll likely come back to it.  On the other hand, it is also a good explanation of why I would never, ever, ever want to be an entrepreneur in real life.  (3 plays)

RATING: 7
When first playing this game I've felt a touch of brilliance and genius by Wallace - similar feeling to that when playing Perikles or Brass.  This feeling has passed now, still it seems like another very solid game from Wallace.

Automobile reminds Power Grid in a sense that it's VERY mathy and reminds Perikles as well - in a sense that there are several elements here that interlock together extremely well.

This is a demanding game for yet another reason - one mistake at the beginning and you have no chance to win.  This is a gamers' game.
Generally, it's a game hard to master - very different gameplay each time and no schematic moves don't make the task easy for the players.

Only this terrible horrible graphics of the board (original edition)... :-/  New edition has its serious flaws too so the Warfrog edition is the best choice anyway.

EDIT: After 8 plays I have enough.  Yeah, there is no one winning path, you have to improvise in each game and try to find the winning recipe anew, but the components (actions) feel repetitive after some time and I've exercised them enough already.

Sold.

RATING: 7.5
initial rating 1 play 3 players

Well I dont think it is the next "Grail" and definitely not Wallaces best game, just my opinion. I thought the theme was fantastic (wife didnt like it however and wanted "shoe" factories) and fit an economic game perfectly with most mechanics relating to theme. 

3 will be the weakest player number by a landslide, the game was NOT scaled properly at all and needs some simple tweaks IMO to play well with 3 unless we played wrong, it was not tight and some actions werent even that useful. I sat on a middle line factory all game and won, never building another pumping cars out like crazy. Yes some things could have been done to prevent it, but thats not the main point.  

Speaking of factories, it did seem strange to me that the luxury factories are somewhat harder to build but less profitable in the end as well. The low end were also less profitable so the middle line are the best to go after and should be the most fought after. I'm not sure about this element but it seemed a bit off to me, it seems it definitely HAS TO BE fought after or I dont see how the other players will compete since they will be less profitable. When fought after, chances are these middle line car producers will end up with some "loss" cubes to compensate for the extra profit being made but I'm not sure it will be enough, maybe. 

Also, as some others have mentioned, with limited options AND choices, the overall scope of strategy is potentially limited, looks to be midweight overall and maybe a touch long for that weight.  

I did actually like the game overlooking some things that were crappy with only 3 players but my rating is tentative (some benefit of the doubt given) until I play 4 or 5 which I know will be better and see how long the next game will take.  For example more factories will be built, which means the loss cubes become more important, building factories down the road more important,  characters which help get rid of loss cubes more important, game tighter overall, etc.    

Update - WAYYYYY better with 5 due to the scaling issue, how do things like this get through playtesting? Sure seems rules need tweaking for 3 players anyway, we had no issues with 5 like we did with 3.

[hr]
2014-09-15

After another play, I can say I enjoyed it a lot more so I’m upgrading its rating. You have many things to think and only 3 actions per round so you have to carefully manage your options. I really like that aspect of the game. It is not clear what your best play is, that is, the game seem to have very interesting decisions (should I open/close a factory?, How many cards should I build, Should I hire sellers?), but I’m afraid they will become obvious with more plays, after all, you NEED to build cards in each round so I’m not really sure if the game is obvious or not. 

The game components and art is good. I think this is best as enjoyed a 4 player game, with more the downtime begins to be very long.

On my dislikes, I found the card demand to be overly random, it can be (with 4 players) from 8 to 20 units for a car market and there is no way to know this ahead of time, so it is uncontrollable and here is when the game is defined. You can of course sell through sellers and Howard but still it is very random for my liking. In both my games the player who correctly guessed the demand won the game as he not only didn’t get any waste cubes, he also made money selling all his cars. If you guess wrong you will lose and it is a guess, not a deduction.

For now Automobile climbs up at a borderline 6.0, but I feel it will come down again with more plays. Automobile also needs some fast players or the game can be very boring.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Has a lot of familiar Wallace motifs - player-driven turn order, low randomness, high borrowing costs, penalties for imprecise play.  However Automobile feels more forgiving than his other titles somehow.  Perhaps this is because the demand is so hard to target precisely that the hope always exists that even a competent owner will misjudge it, or even take a good gamble that backfires.  Or maybe it's because, unlike Age of Steam, incompetent play will probably still be profitable, just a lot less profitable than that of opponents.

Wallace's economic games tend to model supply and demand in straightforward and interesting ways, and I think Automobile is my favorite.  The supply-demand model is also the source of what some players I've played with have criticized about the game.  They say that the correct supply is difficult to target because multiplayer chaos drives the other players' supply.  My answer to that is to pick Chrysler and go last if you're concerned about chaos.  Then other players might still get in your way to produce optimally but you're less likely to take heavy losses due to someone else's bad play.  I appreciate that the randomness comes from the other players (as in Age of Steam) rather than a more arbitrary card mechanism as in Brass.  You can still do whatever you want in Automobile - the chaos effect determines how well it will work out but your action choices aren't randomly restricted.

Theme also is well integrated, up to the standards of previous titles like Brass.  Sure, one can imagine this game being about the birth of the aviation industry, or about diamond mining or something, but the automobile theme is rich and works well for this game.

Played a 3p and thought the game lacked a bit of the tension that I enjoyed so much in my first two 4p games.  That may have been due to my strategy though, as I went heavily into Autobuggies and National 40s, thus avoiding conflict (very profitably) for the first two turns.  It looks like 4 is definitely in this game's sweet spot - I wonder if 5 works or if it devolves into chaos.

Need to get this game to the table again.  I made a bad move in my last game and banished Automobile to the scrap heap for several months.

RATING: 8
Very simply - this game works, thematically and mechanically.  Each player starts with $2000 and he who can turn the most profit after 4 turns of selling cars is deemed the winner!  Simple and elegant.

This is not for the feint of heart as it is very mathy - think Age of Steam or Power Grid, but I prefer Automobile much more than both of those.

An argument can be made that it's a bit long (1.5-2.5 hours depending on experience of group) for what you do.  The heart of the game is that you only get 12 actions (and 4 of those are to produce cars).  Yes, choosing characters, assigning distributors, and executive decisions are also significant, but ultimately you get a measly 12 actions.

From start to finish the games I've played in have flown by smoothly.

Overall, Wallace created another keeper in my book!

RATING: 9
Wallace is at his most original with heavy economic games and I think this is one of his best. His games have developed over time a signature style, with players selecting limited variable actions in order to take best advantage of the mandatory (but not insignificant) decisions about how to make money or collect points. Economic games from most other designers of recent vintage almost invariably involve auctions as a central mechanic. While I love economic games I usually only tolerate the auctions they require, which is probably why Brass and Automobile - being economic games without auctions - really hit home with me. You must be brutally efficient in your choice of actions. With only 3 actions per turn, and one of those almost always being "Produce Cars", there is not a lot of room for error. The tension and doubt while playing is palpable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[b]New Comment[/b]

Automobile is a game I can't see playing more than 1-2 times a year, tops. Though I enjoy the "cut your losses" dynamic, the steep obsolescence of old factories and brands, and the necessity of finding the underserved markets, it just takes way too long to go through the motions. Maybe I've just forgotten what it's like to speed through this in 2 hours with 5 knowledgeable players - though I'm not sure I've ever had the luxury. But this always seems to take a full 3, and unlike some heavy games, this one is quite fragile in regards to pacing. The reason for this is that players are guessing against largely unknown demand and that small over/under errors have large consequences, and when it's all said and done no-one wants to invest three hours in that. Even 2 is questionable. There are so few decisions to make in this game, and many of them are scripted by type but require fine-tuning (timing, exact output) by the player. Unfortunately, this kind of decision-tuning is subject to the "need a new plan" syndrome of games like Tigris and Euphrates, but instead of needing a new plan, players need to recalculate several things simply to change their production output by one or two. It's cumbersome. 

Automobile is a clunky game. I'll give it credit for being different and for actually creating a narrative and sweeping players up in a thematic experience, but the pitfalls are numerous. It's all talk and no follow-through. 

[b]Old Comment[/b]

Automobile shows us just how much ambition Martin Wallace can channel into a game. You have numerous models to choose from. You may commit to full production of those models or do smaller, limited runs. You can diversify between three pricing tiers for your cars, or concentrate on one or two. You can market your models to increase your market share and inflict losses on your opponents. You can declare a "fire sale" to avoid looming losses. You can do a bit of R&D and then ramp up production on a newer, hotter, spiffier brand. You can sell extra models through distributors: just don't leave 'em empty handed or it's more losses. You can shutter obsolete factories to cut your mounting losses (whew!). You can take loans for cash flow and hope the extra production/profit offsets the interest.

Doesn't it sound like there's tons to do in this game? Too bad it's just the opposite. I can't deny that Automobile is an enjoyable experience with its own feel among economic games, but there is shockingly little to do. You have 12 core actions to take over 4 turns. 4 of these must go towards producing cars, so the decision is reduced to timing (when to produce during the turn?) and quantity (how many cars to produce?). At least 3 of these actions will be building factories, so the decision is once again reduced to timing and quantity, with R&D expenditure added in. I've played four times and made my peace with the nature of the game, but this will always compare unfavorably to any game that gives you lots of room to explore and act.

UPDATE: I think this is clearly a 5-player only game. The full board is only used with 5. It's just not nearly as interesting with fewer players. That clouded the initial reception of the game, as our group played a few times with three and four. The first 5P games were a revelation, but after that the game's shortcomings returned in force. It gets by on being different without being that strong, both in individual pieces and in sum total. Ultimately I'm not sure what this game needed to shine. Higher action count? More choices in the Executive decisions phase? More knowledge of demand? All three? I'm willing to play this now and then, but no longer interested in owning it.

RATING: 2
Only played once but I unfortunately have mostly negative things to say about this game. Very unremarkable experience. Choices were often predictable. You have 3 choices per round and one of them almost always HAS to be producing cars (otherwise you earn $0 and give you opponents that much more opportunity to sell their cars without worry of overstock).  There is no competition for actions so you can do whatever you want, whenever you want. The choice order is really obvious and takes little strategy. The salesmen mechanic is simple and easy to take full advantage of. For the entire game I was able to get very single one of my salesmen out where I wanted them to be. There wasn't many left over cars on each round either despite the fact that all 4 of us were buying the maximum # of cars each round on each spot. The number of cars for sale was quite predictable since a majority of the tiles were out each turn and since we knew where the higher value numbers were going we could plan accordingly. I also think there is a significant advantage to going first and second on the final turn (in a 4 player game). Those two players get first crack at an extra full sale and then can still get a second shot at a discount on another car line. The cars on the board are beautiful but the board has flaws. There is no good place to put # markers down when we flip those. The cars we sell didn't fit into the respective boxes on occasion (we played with the car meeples). And why paper money?!? 
Just a big fail in so many aspects.  I completely appreciate Martin Wallace but this is a dud.

RATING: 6
I played 4 times, and then traded my copy away.  Somehow, despite my high enthusiasm, the cool theme, and clever mechanisms, this game didn't fully connecting for me.

I find myself feeling a bit shoehorned by the game.  You get 12 player actions.  Each turn you need to produce cars at least once, so there goes at least 4 of your actions.  Of course to produce cars you need to build a factory, and I'd guess you need to build around 3 (or more) factories during the game, so you have 7 or more of your player actions consumed.  So that leaves you no more than 5 actions to your discretion.

Of those discretionary actions, you might close a factory - but you might not, and aim for the executive decision that allows you to close a factory.  That leaves placing distributors, or the uninteresting 'take 2 cubes'.

Building lots of cars is worth exploring, so you might want to double up on production.  If you do this you'll want to spend more time placing distributors.

Choosing Durant may be interesting for the immediate factory build.  I could see where this action might allow you to spend more energy placing distributors and producing large numbers of cars.


Clearly there is some game space to explore.  But is there enough?  For me, the answer was "no".

RATING: 5
The game is OK and it actually looks close to what you would really do if you were running a car company. Still it's far from being perfect.

First, it is complex and there is a lot of elements connected together. Which made the teaching of the rules last 1 hour. That is way too much.

Second there is a lot of calculation you could do because everything can be planned. It could add downtime if people start calculating a lot.

Third, there is too much control. The designer tries to force the game to go somewhere and I don't like that. For example, it partially tells you what will happen or what you can do every turn of the game. Which makes it impossible for example to play extra turns because they are not on the chart. I would have preferred a less strict control so that it simplifies the rules and makes replay value more interesting.

Finally, you don't make that much money. The people in first place ended up with approximately twice more money than what they started with. Which make game end up pretty close forcing you to calculate even more since a few dollars lost here and there can make you loose the game. 

So I would really wanted to play faster turns but more turns.

---------------------------------------------------

After a second play, again the difference of money between the players is minimal. So you have actually no idea of what made you won the game.

Second, upgrading your cars is not that useful. The only advantage is that it allows you to have priority in the order of the sale. But you can sell cars with sellers, and since sells are done in rounds, you will be selling something. You can also use executive orders to make more sale, so being last in the rank is not that bad. So instead of wasting actions closing factories, you can just build new ones and never close them.

Finally, the new edition is much harder to play because of the components color (I am partially color bind). There is so much chrome that it is much harder to identify the components. Also the character special abilities are not written on the board, so you have to memorize them. So I personally prefer the original version with the wooden parts.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Build factories, produce cars, and meet demand without too much overproduction.  Watch that your factories aren't too far out of date to avoid too many black loss cubes.  It is interesting that as we have played more games to see different strategies emerge and be successful.  Each game has been different to some extent as people produce more than once in a round or at least mix and match when you produce versus taking another action.  Too many times, people get in the rut of building factories or placing distributors on the first two turns and only producing on the third action turn making the game fairly predictable.  Throw a wrench into normalcy and try different things and see how people react.  I found this game to be very simple and streamlined to play for new people - even if they may not be sure how best to proceed.  I've seen slum-lording work well as long as there was a way to dispose of loss cubes, I've seen people push the car track causing people to buy and sell factories like crazy as losses mount, and other strategies succeed.  It is interesting to watch the different strategies collide and interact.

RATING: 4
Why does this remind me of the kind of game I would have played as a piece of shareware in the 1980s? I can just see this thing running in glorious 16-color EGA on a DOS machine.

That's not a damnation of _Automobile_, since I like 1980s shareware games.  But as a board game, this is too dry, and there's too much bookkeeping. (Shuffling money back and forth, counting out little cars, etc.)  Feels almost like a teaching simulation -- "this is why companies are forced to constantly develop product," "this is how slim the profit margins can be in business," etc.  The game requires forward planning and a few tough decisions, but the tension is constantly low.  Nobody struggled to make expenses, occasionally you'd have to tighten your belt and spend less during a turn but this never blindsided a player.  

I note two substantive flaws in the game.  One is that the demand tiles you receive at the beginning of each round effectively tell you nothing.  If I draw a pair of 5s, I have know way of knowing whether my opponents have all 2s, or 4s and 5s, so demand is more or less completely random and unknown each turn.

The second is that that loss cubes are not quite punishing enough, particularly on the last turn.  Obsolete factories should NOT be purchased back for full value -- this created a situation where we all thought we had to shutter old factories to avoid loss cubes, but since the factories were going to be bought back at full value, we lost less money by just taking the loss cubes.


Considering all the above, I see little chance I'll take this for another drive.  

Am I the first to observe that the salesmen in the mid-range selling pool look EXACTLY like damned souls swimming in the lake of fire?  Is this Martin's wry comment on the state of the auto industry, 2009?

RATING: 9
The game distills the essence of decision making as a CEO of an auto company.  What markets to I enter?  How much do I produce?  How much production should I have?  Do I deal with salesman in my network with guaranteed demand or do I take risks on general demand?  Do I advertise?  Do I discount?  How many loans do I take out to finance investments?  

The game has some simple math but very big decisions.  It is a great economic game and one of my favorite Martin Wallance titles.  I adore this game and it is one of the best pure business games available.

RATING: 10
Brilliant game. A true economic simulation. Only 12 actions in the game and they are all crucial and tense(not counting character selection and executive decisions just have just as much tension).  You need to be looking at least a turn ahead with each action (which is hard to do until you have a few games under your belt).

There is a fine line between conservatism and aggression in this game. Too much of either will lose it for you. You CANNOT play it safe and only produce cars are guaranteed to sell. You MUST take some risk and overproduce and find a way to stick your opponents with the losses. Dramtically over produce though, and you'll be the one eating loss cubes.

RATING: 8
After my first play (5 players), I was distinctly perplexed by this game. There is a lot going on and I never felt I had any idea which action would give any benefit. As play progressed, it became obvious that the various moguls had strengths and weaknesses that varied as the turns progressed, but I didn't feel I'd grasped any strategy that would get me into contention, never mind playing for a win. As a result, by the game's end, I felt I'd had an interesting time, but a frustrating one that had not been fun and decided that the game was not for me. I had no further interest...

... until I found myself thinking about it a lot through the days that followed and, by the time a week had passed, I was eager to play again.

I still performed badly, but this time (4 players) I really enjoyed myself. So much so that I'm probably going to buy it. I think I'll give it one more play to make sure but, all things being equal, this game would leap from a paltry 4 to a respectable 8 and, I guess, may even climb higher. I therefore won't rate it until at least my 3rd play.

RATING: 3
On second thought... Automobile is an overrated game imo.

Fun the first (and only) time that I've played... but the game seems to have limited strategic options and is not a game I'm likely to buy even though I like the theme and some aspects of gameplay.

Good thematic feel and mechanics to match but some of the game mechanics and decisions are superfluous.  

If a decision is superfluous, and likely to be made turn after turn, why even bother including it in the game instead of baking it right in (as in allowing players to always perform the action instead of making it a "false choice" among several contending turn options)?

This game had potential, but ruined by poor development imo.

--------------------------------------------

Notes from my one (and likely only) session with this game:

First time played with four players. Rulebook is not clear in a number of places... could really use a better editor.

Anyway, another decent Martin Wallace game. Really like how the mechanics mesh well with the theme (at least compared to other Eurogames). Only 4 turns, and the game is one of those "where the rich become MUCH richer", so very hard to recover from a a single bad mistake or a poor turn.

Decent economic game in some ways, but not convinced this game has long term playability. Specifically, I don't think there will be alot of variability in future plays and each game will be more or less the same. 

There are only 12 actions rounds in the entire game and most players will spend 8 of them building factories and manufacturing cars. That leaves only 4 rounds for something else (likely distribution will take up 2 or 3 action rounds). That's not alot of meaningful choices to make in a 2+ hour game. 

You do get to select a role and there is the executive actions phase, but really, that's not enough given the game length.

RATING: 7
Whoa!

Give this game more than one chance! And try to avoid APers, particularly on your first play. My learning game was a huge turn-off, as the gears were cranking with my opponents, grinding the game to a standstill with an overload of calculations and counting and recounting. I worried, [i]Is this what this game is all about?[/i] The game seemed interesting, but not at all fun.

My second try was MUCH better. Despite shockingly streamlined rules (for Wallace), there's a lot to consider here. Even though many of your actions are scripted, the execution of those actions requires careful attention to what your opponents are doing. I have a slight worry that this will become easier as one gets used to the game, but for now it's really quite challenging. The choice of roles at the beginning of each round is very interesting and is in some ways the heart of the game.

Definitely think this one has legs and overall feels very different from anything else I've played. Solid.

RATING: 10
What an absolutely brilliant game!  A very theme-strong economic game where balance is the key; balancing the elements of the game, evolution of the product market and opponent screwage.

Plenty to think about and some very nice mechanisms, some of which are familiar from Martin's other games.  The really interesting bit for me is working out how to screw over your competition without significant detriment to yourself.  It can be done but involves some risk taking.

A bit of a brain burner?  Could be if you think about it too much but I think it plays just as well if you play with instinct.

Rating based on two plays - one explained by Martin himself at the UK Expo and the second at a local gaming session (for local people.)

RATING: 10
I have a weakness for economic games where players act on a common market which fluctuates due to their own actions. Hence, I kickstarted Brass and while waiting got an opportunity to acquire this less known Wallace title. I was immediately intrigued by the simple yet elegant simulation of the car industry.

Automobile offers the players a careful balance between spending to deliver the top of the line cars and saving to afford the necessary development to continue staying in top. Is this accomplished through complex and fiddly market and development tracks? No, simply draw tiles (looking only at your own tiles) for the demand, spend R&D cubes to build the next generation factories, and supply cars starting from the most modern factories. That's it! Well, there is a bit more but I understand why Wallace considers Automobile one of his best games.

Reviewed at https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1921125
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
My favorite game at The 2009 Gathering!!  What a great simulation of the economic realities that got the American Auto industry into the situation it is in today....we are a car society with ingenuity in design and performance!!  Note:  played the prototype, which I assume is close to final from the smooth manner it played in my two games).

Thanks for yet another wonderful, wonderful challenge and experience, Martin!!  It is amazing how Mr. Wallace can distill R&D, the concept of efficiency, production, demand forecasts(market research) parts, three segments of vehicles (luxury, mid-market and mass market), segment progression and decline, private distributors and retail and wholesale sales into one game.  The progression of the technology and demand trends are layed out in a fascinating way to figure out where your companies best spot is in the marketplace based on all the plans of the other companies and your own ambitions.  The historical characters you can play in in one of four rounds include:  Ford, Kessling, Sloan, Howard, Durham and Chrysler (each with his own unique ability).  There is even a board of directors making executive decisions about both production and selling strategies.

The ability to combine game mechanics and theme is just superb in this one!!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
AWESOME..!!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
One takes the role of an automobile manufacturer in the late 19th century. The game is comprised of four turns. Each turn, the player sees their own demand for middle class, mass market and luxury cars, which helps them plan for their production. Each turn, a player chooses a role which gives them a bonus. They then take 3 actions to put down factories, produce cars, shut down factories, or put more salesmen into play. 

Factories are staged around the board so that older factories become obsolete, and will take "loss" cubes. Similarly, you earn loss cubes for unsold cars, and unused salesmen.

This i a typical Martin Wallace offering with tough decisions, and loss cubes to worry about. It's an okay game to play, and clocks in around 2 hours.
