RATING: 7
It's an economic game with lots of features typical of Wallace's games (loans you have to suffer all game long brutal competition, "realistic" market simulation, etc).

It's a medium to heavy game: it's not difficult to make the calculations, but it's hard to predict what your rivals do, and this as important as your own actions: a simple unexpected action of one of your rivals can make you lose a lot of money, and then the game.

Even if this edition doesn't have wooden cars, I like the quality of the components (yes, I know it includes papernotes). The 20's throwback graphic design is quite elegant, even if the part of the boardgame that it's not cars is certainly improvable.

I don't like the part where you lose the game only because one of your competitors decided to reduce drastically their prices and there's nothing you can do about it, it's too harsh and "unfair" for me. But the experience is worth it. I would certainly play it again.

First impression [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/173073/item/3622235#item3622235]here[/url].

RATING: 8
The game is on a highly abstracted level. It actually felt almost a bit too abstract for my taste. There is pretty much only a development track around the game board and few places for distributors. Rest is more or less memory aid. The game itself is interesting with only 12 actions during the game. You build/close factories (production lines really), produce cars and try to sell them for profit and hope there is enough demand. It's a game of economics and I liked it but it might be better with more players as 3 felt too few. Update: After playing with 4 (and correct rules) it seems to be better this way. Would work mechanically well with 5 too but in our group the length of game would be too long.

RATING: 7.5
規則:
*將所有需求板塊放到袋中，將總裁行動列捕滿(前三格一白圓片，第四格兩灰圓片，剩下一灰)，放對應腳色板塊在對應腳色格，兩個黑色圓片一個放回合軌一個放階段軌起始位置
*每個人拿一個顏色的所有配件，2000圓，3/4/5人拿5/4/3白色方塊，隨機起始或年輕起始，放顏色圓片到順位軌
*遊戲總共進行四輪，每輪有九個階段移動階段標記紀錄之，最後錢多的獲勝
1.抽需求板塊，第一輪抽1，之後都抽2，此為隱藏資訊，根據不同輪預測不同車型的需求
2.照順位軌選擇腳色，移動到腳色板塊上並拿對應腳色板塊跟上面顯示的白色方塊，之後階段順位都照腳色上到下
>Ford 支付對應價格蓋一個工廠或配件工廠在有你工廠的地點
>Kettering 給你三個白色方塊無其他能力
>Sloan 在階段8付款前棄掉手上一半以上的黑色方塊(向上取整棄)
>Howard 賣車階段先賣兩台給他，使用正常價格
>Durant 馬上用正常規則蓋一個工廠在空格上
>Chrysler 階段8付款前棄掉同等回合數的黑色方塊
3.行動階段  所有玩家照腳色順位輪流行動，總共會有三輪，每人等於有三次行動機會
*貸款 不夠錢時放一個玩家標記到貸款格拿500，最多拿兩次，在階段8時要付50的利息，遊戲最後每個要還600
>蓋工廠  放工廠到圖板的周遭格，只能從最左上順時鐘蓋，支付上面的金額，最多一次蓋兩棟在同一格，如果新蓋的超過目前最新的，超過幾格付幾格累積的白色方塊(3格要付1+2+3共6顆)跟對應該格的金錢(每棟)，每格只能一個玩家蓋
*零件工廠要蓋在有工廠的位置，移動500，關閉工廠後回手，每輛車-20
*每個位置最多三工廠+一個零件工廠
>放銷售員 放最多三個銷售員到圖板銷售格(三種車型三格)，沒限制，正常來說會放中間
>拿兩個白色方塊
>生產車  所有你有工廠的位置產車，根據生產表對應等級的車根據工廠數可產一個範圍的數量，範圍內產幾台自選，也可選擇不產，總上限28台
>關閉工廠 關閉一個點的所有工廠，工廠回手放置關閉標記，並且棄掉手上一半的黑色方塊(向上取整)，每個工廠拿回該格-100的錢(零件工廠拿400)，如果該格有車不移除，如果關閉工廠標記不夠用，從最舊的位置拿
4.賣車給howard 選擇該腳色的人必須賣兩台車給他，使用圖板標示的原價
5.銷售員賣車 照腳色位置順位，在銷售格內有銷售員的輪流一次移動一個銷售員到左邊的銷售方格，並拿一台對應的生產車放置賣高價位(強迫要做)，左移時可上下移動一格，且第一輪只能使用開頭是1的，第二輪可使用1跟2的，之後可用全部，此行動執行到所有人跳過為止，所有玩家拿錢且所有銷售員右移回大方格
*剩下的銷售員回手，每回一個拿一個黑色方塊
6.總裁決定 照腳色順位每人可選擇行動，直到所有人跳過
>關閉工廠 只能有一個玩家選擇，同上面行動的關閉工廠
>買廣告  從最左邊開始花對應方塊拿白色圓片放到自己有車格上，每格最多一個
>拿折扣圓片 自己選擇那2或1個折扣圓片放在自己有車格上(兩片的話要同格)，不能放在高檔車上
>跳過 移動順位圓片到順位軌的最前面格
7.根據需求賣車  所有玩家展示需求板塊並放到對應格(第二回合高的放中階，三四回合顛倒)，三四回合多抽一個需求板塊放高階，第四回合低階再多抽一個，之後車子從最先先進的格往後一次一台放到對應的賣車格，一輪後如果需求數字還沒滿足再重最新進的來一輪值到達到數字總數台或車賣完，所有玩家根據車放的位置拿錢，沒賣出的每台拿一顆黑色方塊，所有車回手，廣告跟折扣圓片回對應位置
*廣告板塊格可多放一台，折扣圓片每個也可多放一台，只要有折扣圓片全部放在低價區
8.損失跟利息  三種不同的工廠分開計算，每種類型工廠前面有幾座先進的工廠進拿幾顆黑色方塊(包括關閉的)，大顆的價值五顆小的，拿完後根據腳色能力可棄掉一部份，然後根據回合數付方塊*10*回合數的錢，每個貸款付50元利息
9.回合結束 所有需求板塊丟回袋中，腳色板塊放回，車收回，移動回合標記
*遊戲結束時所有場上工廠拿對應該格的價錢，然後支付貸款，最後錢多獲勝，平手比順位靠前

***兩人變體***
*每人起始6白方塊，隨機抽兩個人物板塊放人物順位區，抽兩個放玩家順位區，使用一個中立的銷售員顏色，白色廣告圓片跟灰色促銷圓片最後一格不用，移除2個5，1個4的需求板塊
2.選腳色時不能選有板塊的位置
3.行動階段放銷售員時同時放1個中立銷售員在該格
5.每次輪到你移動銷售員或跳過時，都可以移動一個中立銷售員到左邊佔格(一樣可向上或下一格)
9.將人物順位區的板塊移除，順位區的放到人物順位區，然後再抽兩個出來放到順位區，每個玩家拿一個關閉工廠標記放到自己目前最前面工廠的前方空位

RATING: 7
It seems a game of continual planning for potential disasters, calculating how far you can push things. There's some nice interleaving of mechanisms, starting with the character choices which dictate turn order and special actions. You want to go first to sell cars more easily in the first sell phase, but you want to go last to build the most advanced factory, see what the others are building and therefore minimise risk and sell cars more easily in the second sell phase. Once played, the theme brings it all together well. But that doesn't make the choices you're faced with any easier, especially when you add in the obsolescence feature which forces people to continually sell off and build new factories to re-gain better position in the sell-off pecking order. It's one for the business game lovers out there, not one for the thrill seekers. And I can't help but think after a couple of plays that too much of your game is dependent on turn order at particular times (and what people leave you in that turn order) and the vagaries of the semi-random sale number generator process that may well determine whether you sell for profit or take a loss, which can be a fair sized swing in your money and therefore your result, with only 4 turns in the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Played the pre-production game several times at TGoF-09, I found that aggressive production and marketing paid off, but this has to be a group-culture thing. In the games I played, people tended to be overcautious, losing profits from under-production, which is almost as bad as over-producing.

Automania posed similar problems, but there the decisions were revealed simultaneously, making it a pure guessing game. Here the sequential nature of actions and the ability to effect the player order  confront the players with more subtle choices. Go early and get first choice of the marketing options, or go late and react to the other players' production decisions

RATING: 8
Wallace è una garanzia, e anche stavolta sforna delicatissime meccaniche a orologeria in un gioco che rappresenta alla perfezione la definizione di "Gamers' Game", un gioco pensato per i giocatori assidui. All'insegna della scarsità di risorse e di moltissima interazione diretta, i giocatori non vincono se producono a basso rischio, ma perdono sonoramente se sbagliano i conti mettendo sul mercato troppe auto. Un gioco senza pietà: una o due azioni sprecate nelle dodici complessive pesano tantissimo. Inoltre, i margini sulle auto sono bassissimi. E' molto ben trasposto il meccanismo di scelta dei personaggi, eccezionale il sistema di corsa alla tecnologia, che obbliga gli ultimi a investire in ricerca per non essere fuori dai giochi. Le meccaniche della vendita mi hanno lasciato più indifferente: molto poco rischio calcolato (conoscere la tua tessera mercato non ti aiuta a farti un'idea della domanda totale) e difficile farsi un'idea di come andranno le vendite (tra venditori e cilindretti bianchi/grigi anche l'ultimo del turno potrebbe piazzare tutta la sua produzione prima degli altri). Forse una partita a cinque rende il tutto più caotico: penso che in quattro o in tre il gioco diventi più strategico. A trovare qualcosa che non (mi) va, i marchi di fabbrica di Wallace: l'estrema bruttezza della grafica della plancia, forse la peggiore in assoluto della sua produzione (ma i componenti di legno sono notevoli) e l'impianto delle regole molto indigesto (e pesantemente basato sui calcoli). Infine, complici i due punti di cui sopra, nonostante i disegni davvero osceni delle auto e qualche finezza (l'andamento della domanda del mercato delle auto, davvero ben pensata), l'asciuttezza del gioco mi lascia un retrogusto di astrattone. Comunque, un gioco che tutto sommato non delude le aspettative.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is one of the better supply-and-demand type games that I've played.  This game is full of agonizing decisions.  You need to invest in factories to produce cars, but if you buy a factory that quickly becomes outdated, you could start raking in the losses.  You want to produce and sell as many cars as you can, but if you overproduce, not only is the excess wasted, but you take a loss for every unsold car.  Distributors can be a good way to get around your competition, but if you hire too many distributors, they too will take losses.  Every decision can either make you a significant profit or put you in a deep hole (and sometimes both!), giving you tons to think about.

(4 plays)

RATING: 7
A Wallace-game without a map, but still with all the characteristics of the designer.

Automobile is a economic game where demand is hard to estimate, and where reckless moves will cost you dearly.

There is lots of interaction and watching your opposing players make their moves is just as exiting as doing your own.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
After one bad experience caused by a few major rules mistakes the second game was better. 

It's a business game with a car building theme: build factories to produce cars and then sell them in the market. Demand is unpredictable within certain boundaries and if you overproduce you'll be stuck with the unsold cars.

It seems important to manage your money carefully (duh), not to overbuild production capacity and to try and stay ahead of the demand trends. 

One of my problems with it, is that 95% of the decision making seems to happen in the character selection phase, because that's where turn order is determined. Also, there aren't a lot of actions during a full game so it feels that your decisions don't matter as much as they should.

It's not a bad game, but I didn't enjoy it as much as I expected. It's very calculational and at the same time fairly chaotic and unpredictable in the selling phase. 

I won't refuse to play it, if in the right mood, but it's far from being a personal favorite.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The Martin Wallace games I've played - Steam, Brass, and now Automobile - all seem to rate an 8 for me. Just the tiniest bit too heavy and unforgiving and mathy for my tastes, but wonderful, engrossing, cerebral designs. It's hard to say which one I like best, and they're still different enough that I'm glad I own all three.

Automobile is a game that asks two questions: How many cars am I going to sell this round, and how do I manage my loss cubes? All tactical decisions follow from those strategic questions. Where, when and how many factories am I going to build? What CEO will I take? &c. You have to be mindful of step 6 right when you start at step 1. Everything works together in a web of pushes and pulls that make figuring out what you want to do tremendously interesting.

Player interaction is everything. Every move a player makes is an aggression. Someone builds a factory? That means they plan to build more cars, thus sell more cars, and take a bigger portion of market demand, which means that you can sell less, so maybe should produce less, so maybe shouldn't build a factory yourself. But that means giving an edge to the other player, so maybe you do want to build a factory and prepare to fight over the market.

Like the other Wallace games, Automobile is a constant struggle that requires constant calculations and risk estimates, without ever giving you the sense that you're doing well and can relax a bit. If that's something you don't mind, if you like to work for satisfying victories more than friendly evenings where everyone is pleased with how they're doing, I can give my highest recommendations for Automobile.

RATING: 6
Martin Wallace has managed to make the mechanics fit perfectly with the theme and it really feels like running a business. I like that you are doing well if you double your money over the game - unlike the unrealistic economies in Power Grid, Acquire and others. It's also a good mixture of calculation and chance. Just like in real business, you don't know the exact market conditions and sometimes you just have to go with your gut.

UPDATE 
I was blown away by Automobile at first, but all my plays of it came within the first month and I haven't really wanted to pull it out since. The problem is the jarring contrast between the detailed profit and loss calculations needed and the randomness that can render them meaningless. I think the demand tile mechanic needs fixing, because the tiles you see simply aren't enough to tell you anything useful. Playing a lot of Brass online later in the year reaffirmed how much better it is than 'mobile, and I traded it away in December '09.

RATING: 8
Automobile is surprisingly suspenseful and has a lot of different interesting choices.  I've read people who think that there are not very many choices to make when playing Automobile and I completely disagree.  Each decision of what to make and where to spend your actions is a dynamic process.  Additionally, each decision that each other player makes greatly affects what decisions you can and should make.

I have only played Automobile with five players, and I can imagine that it would play suboptimally with less than five.  This game is one of the few games that is all about player interaction that I still find enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Players build factories for various automobile models and sell their vehicles through distributors and direct sales. Building a new model with low demand is costly, as is maintaining an obsolete factory. Tiles secretly drawn each round determine adjustments in public demand. Then, role selection of auto industry pioneers provide benefits to each player -- e.g. Henry Ford allows you to buy an extra factory each turn. During the action phase, players can then: build or shut down factories (brand new models require R&D cubes), place distributors to sell your cars, take R&D cubes, or produce (following strict guidelines for the factories owned. During the Executive Decisions phase, players get bonuses such as shutting down an extra factory or using R&D cubes to launch a reduced price sale. Finally, cars are sold to the general public, as demand tiles are revealed and, naturally, the newest ,odels are the first to sell. Loss cubes are assigned for unsold cars, older modls getting the most cubes. A deep game requiring difficult mental calculation, a balance of aggression and restraint, and the ability to effectively forecast demand.

3+.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
--Jun2009-- Despite coming in Dead Last, I rather enjoyed my first attempt.  I was tremendously amused by the ripples set up by various decisions.  It's not at all clear that I'll be able to get this to the table regularly, but I think it will play reasonably quickly with a bit of practice, and - perhaps more importantly - some improved play will prove possible with that practice. 

It's an interesting system:  I think there's room for having more information about the incoming demand - but I don't know how it could work cleanly.  Perhaps one could trade a R&D cube or two for a glimpse of neighbours' demand tiles?  It's not quite right:  the economy of R&D cubes is tighter than that, and there's not a lot of excess left.

After a third play, I can see some new opportunities for havoc that I hadn't noticed the first time out.  Very happy to have bought it.

--Feb2010-- I think the comparison to _Lokomotive Werks_ is overwrought; they seem like quite divergent exercises to me, even though there's common shared themes of demand and obsolescence.

All in all it boils down to the ability to do multiple arithmetic in your head (preferably quickly), and your luck in the demand draw (of which the luxury cars is a complete crap shoot). That said, it's an interesting ride with some interesting choices, but that's just me - I don't mind these types of games. There's a tendency for play to slow down as calculations are made by each player for each scenario. Using my imagination the theme is well meshed with the mechanics because of the best bit in the game - the characters and their historically based and well balanced skills. I don't see my rating for this increasing, and while I'm tempted to nudge it up to an eight because of the great board design and well written rules, I feel that would be meta-rating, so it's a solid seven for me.

I hate, hate, hate the new Mayfair board and cardboard bits. Three different shades of boring in my opinion. Where the original board has all the important information standing out, the new one has everything blurring into one mess of brown and grey. The dumbest thing they did was remove the text on the character selections. Why? To make the game harder to play? Tastes differ I guess, but in my opinion it's just awful. Seek out the original Warfrog edition if you get the chance.

RATING: 7
Straight up, this game is merely about being efficient beyond a fault, since the tension comes from the board, not from other players. I usually don't like these games, but Automobile is fun, engaging and elegant enough that I am willing to play this over and over.

This game is all about financial blackjack. Produce too few cars and that's a lost profit opportunity. But even worse, produce too many, and you're saddled with loss cubes. Go as close as you can to the limit, but don't saturate.

This is the lightest of Martin Wallace's heavy games, because it plays comfortably in 2 hours, it has no interesting spatial element, and you only have 12+ actions to take, of which many are somewhat scripted. But the simplicity of the 3x4 action structure lends this game an air of elegance. It means every action you take is very, very important, and just the slightest mistake will leave you with regret for days. It also means it's easy to recount what you did, and troubleshoot what you can do better next time.

This game provides a great lesson in investment. Should you take a loan? Can you make a good profit from your new factory? Are you going to take risks and try to sell in bulk with high margins, or are you going to take a more cautious approach?

4+3

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
If you liked Tinner's Trail but thought it was too light, this might be for you: it feels a bit like TT but with added depth/complexity. It's a good economics game ... I just wish I could figure it out!

Compared to TT (similarities):
- 4 Turns, multiple steps each turn.
- VERY true to the theme: you face decisions about how many/what type of cars to make, when to make them, how to sell them better and beat the competition, how much effort (actions) to spend on research, how to minimize losses (from inefficient factories and dealerships/distributors), etc.
- you have some turn order manipulation going on: turn order changes, based on when you pass the previous turn
- you produce stuff. In Automobile it's cars vs. copper/tin, of course, but you also have more options (type of car, specific factory does matter).
- sell stuff. But not all that you produce -- only what is determined by the demand tiles. And you may not get to sell everything -- you _are_ competing with other players directly here. Also, demand is somewhat random (although you do have limited partial knowledge) so you need to take steps to ensure you can sell what you produce.
- all of the above is modified by which Character/Role/special action (TT) you choose, which are also true to theme.

Others have compared it to Lokomotive Werks, which I've only played once:
- cars vs. trains
- similar overall premise/theme: build factories of varying technology levels, produce stuff, sell based on (randomly determined) demand. In Loko, though, the demand is known at the beginning of the turn. In Auto, you have to deal with more uncertainty -- and there are several ways to do so (build higher-tech factories, control number of cars you produce, establish a distributor network, choose Howard, take multiple executive actions).
- Whereas Loko seems very bare bones, almost as dry as a complete abstract, Automobile seems to be a much fuller, complete experience. There is a LOT more going on in Automobile (roles, extra actions, etc.), in a very well-integrated and thematic way.
- Loko, in my one play, also seemed extremely brutal -- and in an almost random/unpredictable way (although I suspect that's just because of lack of experience with the game?). Still, I think I need to go back and try Loko again just to get a better feel for it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A well produced game that is visually striking and is dripping with theme.  I enjoy economic games and, after my initial play, I liked it to a certain extent and my rating could actually improve.  I have a couple issues though.  First, we had 12 actions in the game (not counting choosing a role, which seems rather minor).  Everyone uses 4 of those for production, so that leaves only 8 real decisions in the entire game, and four of those are almost always to build  factory, resulting in four actions in the entire game that you may use differently than your opponents.  That's not much for such a long game.  You can argue that there are meaningful decisions within all the actions, such as how many cars to produce during the production phase.  However, that leads to my second issue.  Normally you want to produce to the maximum your factory will allow, so then you have to hope you don't get unlucky with the random tile pulls.  I think this is the most critical aspect that determines the winner.  In other words, all the time building and planning during a two hour game comes down to random tile draws.  In our game, for example, the mid-sized cars at the end came out unusually low, so I missed out on selling one car, the difference between first and third place.  Lastly, selling your factories back at the end for full price makes no economic sense.  It seems there should be some type of sliding scale depending upon the age of the factory.  Maybe I'm being too picky here and some of my observations could prove to be incorrect upon further plays. Perhaps the luck element representing consumer demand is a realistic simulation of the fickle buying public!  In short, I still like the game, but there aren't enough meaningful decisions and it may often boil down to random elements to determine the winner.  Then again, I seem to be one of the few who does not like Brass, so perhaps my rating should be taken with a grain of salt.

RATING: 7
Thoughts after one play - approachable but deep game that will play quickly, mechanics work very well with theme. Demand,  production and sale models very clever. Purchased UK Expo (could n't wait for my shop copies....)

2nd game - Brain burner, mistakes in last round can be brutal. Interested to see how demand works with less than five players. 

3rd game - 4 players. Demand still close to mean. Interesting psychology at Exec. decisions - One person taking a bonus/discount cube sparks a rush.
Interaction and turn order key, in some circumstances worth producing less to avoid exec.decisions then grabbing first  role selection, however by reducing the supply you allow others to avoid spending r&d cubes.

Interesting contest between the inefficient producers 2 spots x 1 fac vs.the 2/3 factories. They had economy of scale and actions i had better ability to  sell.

4th game 3 player and more options open and also more obvious effect of actions on other players, so
you have more control and can game it a bit more than with 4 or 5. Think 4 is optimum number? 

5th 6th games. Feels poker like in that it pays to do the  opposite of the concensus, tight v loose and loose v tight. Also that over production can force opponents to follow suit. Conservative play rewards over producers especially 3/4 player (Edit - no accurate play)

7th game. Fairly brutal to new players. Furthest i have seen players build, newplayers tooscared ofloss cubes?

12th game first of 2010 (August) and burning out in last 2 games played in 2009. Still a great game, v interactive but indirectly

Having played Vinhos a couple of times it always makes me want to play  Automobile which is a far superior business game

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
The good: Every decision you make is meaningful and has broad implications for your future and impacts the other players dramatically.  – I love the tough decisions, what car to research and build, how many distributors to place, role selection, how many cars to build, all of these decisions have implications on for your future.  

The bad: Every decision you make is meaningful and has broad implications for your future and impacts the other players dramatically.   – The trouble with that is you need to add the information of the last move and recalculate your move in order to optimize your move.  This need to reanalyze once it gets to your turn causes the game to drag a little and the amount of information you need to process can be daunting.

The depth of each aspect of the game is shocking in its simplicity.   I will just describe one action as an example of the simplicity vs. depth:  Deciding what car to build has layers of strategy, save or use your research cubes, if you use all your cubes to jump ahead a couple cars, it only serves to allow the players behind you to get ahead of you cheaply.  That is in addition to what type of car you can build, how many factories, how many cars you can build (with and without a loan).  All these decisions have to consider, what markets you are already in, where your distributors are, what markets your opponents are in, how much money they have, and what markets you EXPECT them to be in.  

All in all, frustratingly deep, annoyingly long, but immensely fulfilling.  I give it an 8.5.  

***I am amending the annoyingly long part. That is only with the 'wrong crowd'.  AP prone players can make this drag. 8.5 is still appropriate but this should play fairly quickly if you can nudge the AP prone players along a little bit.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Not Wallace's best, as it can suffer from pretty severe AP a some points, and the game lasts about 30% longer than it really should because the car sales portion of the game can sometimes need to be moved through step by step (sometimes the sales portion moves quickly though because there are plenty of buyers).  But the game itself is quite good with lots going on, and while you can take risks, you're not  subject to wild swings of luck (although that very thing is what makes it subject to some turns of severe AP). 

Typical of many of Wallace's games, to play well, you must actually take the time to analyze your relative positioning amongst players before you can analyze your best move.  This is good for overall competitive play, but bad when any of your players is impatient.

I'm still undecided on whether the game holds enough interest to justify the play-time.

RATING: 8
The first thirty years of US auto-making abstracted down to a mere 12 actions.

Our first play was probably too conservative (all cars were sold; nobody used Reduce Price markers; I came 2/5 without using any distributors). Now we have a bit more of a feel for things, I'm hoping in future games we'll push capacity to the limit in search of ever greater profits.

Second play (different group) was much more vicious. Unsold cars just about every turn. Plenty of loss points shared around. Much more cutthroat, but also more arithmetic involved.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
Another Wallace game that feels like a Wallace game. Not always a bad thing, and in this case it makes something that is better than average, but not something that I'd play frequently.

I do find some of the choices interesting, especially how your actions depends on what other people have chosen more so than what comes up on the board (as the only random element is the demand tiles). It's similar to other Wallace games where you have to spend money to make money, in this case by buying factories and making cars and then selling said cars for profit, all the while making sure you don't make too many cars or have too many distributors trying to sell said cars. Feels a little too mathy and calculating, especially near the end when you have to decide what type of cars would be best to build.

First game of 4 players I ended in first place with a score of $3930 (second place had $3900). Would have probably done better but I went "balls to the walls" and took a double loan and way over produced just for fun.

Like all the Wallace games I've played, it seems the best strategy is to get as close as possible to bankrupt by using all your money to maximize production and then using that to sling shot yourself back to top. That's what I did, and most people though I was going to lose by the end of the second turn.

After second play I like the game slightly more, mainly because it is unique and I can't think of another game like it. I don't understand the board because it is almost impossible to get around it due to R&D costs, but that's a minor issue. It seems that mass producing one automobile that another person isn't capitalizing on is the way to win, especially the cheap cars.

RATING: 8
It does a great job of capturing the uncertainty of business decisions -- will I be able to sell all that I produce?  will I leave something on the table by not producing enough?  What market segments look good, and how will my competitors react to that?  Yeah, it is basically an elaborate game of chicken, but that is the point -- it isn't a delicate math puzzle of an economic game, it is about finding the best way to slap around your competitors and muscle them out of the prime markets.  Not one of my all-time favorites, but it is certainly intriguing and worthy of a number of plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[thing=39351][/thing] is a medium weight economic game by Martin Wallace, in which the purpose is to make and sell cars in the late 1890 and early 1900. It is, also, a really tight game: only four rounds, with 3 actions (plus a few more decisions) per round.

At the beginning of each round, players secretly take, from a bag, tiles with numbers (from 2 to 5), which will form the demand of cars for that round - but the player only knows the amount he took, and must guess what the overall demand for cars will be. Then each player, in turn order, select a patron - a renowned personality from the auto industry - that will provide some sort of benefit - for example: Ford allows the player, when building factories, to build one extra (up to three); Sloan take away part of the debt (black tokens) of the player; Howard ensures the sell of two cars; and so on.

Then comes the actions part of the round: each player will do 3 actions, one at a time; some of the actions available are:
- build one or two factories (first the player must decide which type of car the factory will made, paying research tokens if the type is more advance than the current type available); 
- producing cars. There are a minimum and a maximum that must be respected, depending on the number of factories the player has for the type of car;
- send sellers (up to three) to the auto show;
- gain research tokens (three). These tokens are used to make more advanced cars and to advertise them.

After each player do his three actions, the sellers sent to the auto show now must be allocated accordingly to the type of car (popular, medium or deluxe) they will sell. Only a given number of sellers can be allocated for each type of car - this number rises in later rounds.

Then comes the executive decisions, which are basically three:
- close factories (gaining back 50% of the price paid for them and returning half of the debt tokens);
- advertise (this assures the sell of more cars, but it must be paid with research tokens);
- do a sale (also helps to sell cars, but for a reduced price);
- pass (this defines the turn order for the next turn).

Now comes the sell of cars accordingly to the demand tiles. The numbers are added up, and everyone sells cars in order of the type of cars, with the more advance models of cars selling first, it is also now that the advertise and sales help with the selling of cars. For example: it is the first round, and the demand are for 12 cars (tiles 2, 2, 3 and 5). Player 1 made 3 cars of the Maxwell Model L (the most advanced model). Player 2 have 4 cars of the Franklin Model A (the second most advanced). Player 3 has also 4 cars to sell, but from the Oldsmobile Curved Dash model (the third most advanced). Finally, Player 4 has 2 cars to sell from the Duryea model (the older model). Player 2 and 3 both have paid for advertisement (one token of advertisement for each). The sell of the 12 cars will occour like this:
- Player 1 sells one car;
- Then Player 2 sells two cars (one normal, one per the advertisement token);
- Then Player 3 sells two cars (one normal, one per the advertisement token);
- Then Player 4 sells one car.
A total of 6 cars were sold. Still 6 to go. So the selling start again:
- Player 1 sells one car;
- Then Player 2 sells two cars (one normal, one per the advertisement token);
- Then Player 3 sells two cars (one normal, one per the advertisement token);
- Then Player 4 sells one car.
All 12 cars from the demand have been sold. All for full price (no one used a sale token). Player 1 was left with still one car to sell.

After the sell part comes the debt/losses. Players will receive one black token for each car not sold, plus one for each seller that couldn't be allocated, plus one for each model of the same type (popular, medium or deluxe) of car more advanced than the type he has to sell. Each black token cost an amount of money to be paid by the player in this phase - the amount increases as the rounds advance.

The round then ends. Basically that is it. Rinse and repeat three more times and the game is over! The player with the most money by the end is the winner.

[thing=39351][/thing] is, as I said, a really tight economic game: there are so few actions, that each must count - no decision can be made lightly, and this is particularly true if the game have 4 or 5 players - the demand, the order of the sells, the use of sellers, advertisement, sales, basically everything has a keener edge with higher player count. The game with 3, though, can be lacking: often the players will mostly just stay out of the way one from the other (since there are three types of cars, in the later rounds it is possible for every player be specialized in one type). My recommendation, then, is to play with 5 - BGG is right that the is best with this number.

The deluxe edition is a treat: the tiles and tokens are thick enough for each to count as two from other games. The paper money is simply beautiful and sturdy. The board... well, it does its job, and is kind of nice, but there is something (maybe is too busy) that prevents me from saying that it is pretty - it is, though, well done, as everything else.

A full game can be played around 90 minutes, even with 5 players. It can be more, of course, but I found [thing=39351][/thing] to be a quicker sort of game, without much room for downtime (but it can induce to some serious AP) - the actions available are simple to understand, the resolution for them is clear.

[thing=39351][/thing] is just a great economic game - fast, fun, mean, hard -, and could be a fine addition to any collection.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Played once with 3 people.  I think it would play better with 4 or 5.  The distributors do not scale based on the number of players, so with 3 the game feels less competitive than it probably should.  We had no problem selling all our vehicles.  Less meaty than it seems at first glance.  I expect if they had kept the distributor map in place instead of abstracting it, the game would have felt more meaty.  But I expect it would not have added much to the gameplay while adding greatly to the time.  As it is, you get a Wallace game that might be able to be played somewhat quickly once folks know the rules.  Like many Wallace games, I probably need to play it many times before I understand the interactions and the strategies.  Unfortunately, the game itself was not that impressive to me otherwise.  Rules were a little hard to follow and the example turns had some pictures which mis-matched the actual actions, so that added to the confusion.  Rating probably will go up with more plays.  I just don't know whether I will play it much more.  Too many good games competing for play time.

A 3-5 player game that bears a modern setting when compared to most of Wallace’s releases. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Automobile is a 3-5 player game that bears a modern setting when compared to most of Wallace’s releases. Players are competing in the U.S. auto industry in the early 20th century, purchasing factories that turn out low-, medium- and high-valued vehicles, starting with the 1893 Duryea and moving through history from there.

Each player knows a portion of the market demand each round and must make his purchasing and manufacturing decisions based on the information. Players can fund distributors across the country, but if they don’t supply distributors with vehicles to sell, they go bankrupt, taking your investment with them. Alternatively, players can drop the prices on their cars to move their market share, or even temporarily improve sales rates at the cost of research. Special action spaces are available that give a player a one-turn special ability with the actions provided by Ford, Durant, Kettering, and others some what related to their actual business history.

As newer models make their way onto the market, they sell at the expense of the older models. Older factories give inefficiency cubes as time passes, encouraging you to keep pace with technology.

To get money, you need to build cars with your factories, but if you build more than there is demand theylose not only the money spend to make them, but gain inefficiency cubes that hurt them for the rest of the game. Whoever manages their car factories the best over this 120-150 minute game will win.

RATING: N/A
So happy I got to play another Wallace game. Automobile is a tight economic game about manufacturing and selling cars. I can see what the winning strategy is though, which doesn't make this one something that will hold my interest log-term but I'd play it a lot more with strong players. The game is dependent on the skill of your opponents and what they do. I played with strong players the first time and went heavy into manufacturing the brown and silver cars but lost to the more expensive gold factories people had. I don't think the game can be won with high volume brown and silver car sales alone, unless no one goes into gold sales, which is unlikely. Love the turn order mechanic/specialty role selection at the beginning of each round as well.

RATING: 5
Although less convoluted and fiddly than your typical Martin Wallace game, the flip side is that there seems to be less going on and room for strategic exploration is very limited; the game is fairly procedural and lacks dynamism. My concern is that the game among experienced players will come down to who guessed the demand right most often, i.e. who managed to sell the most with the fewest unsold cars left (and preferable to guess right early, as the extremely high value of liquidity basically makes this an economic snowball game). The partial knowledge of demand is a complete gimmick and could have been removed from the game or otherwise implemented better (e.g. allow you to also look at the demand tile belonging to the player on your left). Clearly the most interesting decision is what character to choose; from there decisions are generally obvious and largely uninteresting. This cements my view of Wallace as a designer with interesting ideas but a flawed sensibility. Even my favorite games of his I enjoy despite some detail or other that bothers me.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After one play (hell, after one ROUND), I saw something incredible in the design, and after it was over, I wanted to talk about it, play it again, and try something different to see if I could do any better.  This is a tight, agonizing little game, played over 4 turns, for a total of 12 actions, and trying to decide when to do what is what gives the game its tension.  At first, I thought that the game might have a scripted start once players got to be familiar with the game, but I think the demand tiles, and the corollary loss cubes, will prevent that from being the case from turn two forward.  The economic engine is easy to see in this game, so it's easy to tell if you're making a profit, and in all honesty, it's likely that everyone will; the question is whether or not YOU can be more profitable than anyone else.  It's definitely a game that gives players lots of options, with a limited time to do them all, meaning that you will NEVER get to do everything you really want to do on a turn.  It's tight, agonizing, and interesting, but if you're going to play with 3 players, you might want to doctor the executive decision chart, since it seems too easy for players to get a hold of the bonus sale markers.  What can I say?  It's Wallace at his best.

1 play: 3 players
1 play: 4 players
